## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

1. **Intensional Fallacy**: The fallacy of assuming that the grammatical object of an expression denotes a proposition when it does not.
2. **Propositional Attitudes**: Mental states such as doubt, belief, and intention, whose objects are propositions.
3. **Necessity**: The property of a statement or identity that holds in all possible worlds.
4. **Analytic Truth**: A statement that is true in virtue of the meanings of its constituent concepts.
5. **Natural Kind**: A category of objects defined by their micro-characteristics, which are predictively and explanatorily fecund.

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

1. **Grammatical Complement**: The part of a sentence that completes the meaning of a verb or preposition.
2. **Logical Complement**: The part of a sentence that denotes a proposition and completes the meaning of a verb or preposition in a logical sense.
3. **Contingent Identity**: The incoherent concept of an identity that holds only in some possible worlds.

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts

1. **Grammatical vs. Logical Complements**: The text distinguishes between the grammatical structure of a sentence and its logical structure.
2. **Analytic vs. Synthetic Truths**: The text distinguishes between truths that hold in virtue of the meanings of their constituent concepts and those that do not.
3. **Necessary vs. Contingent Truths**: The text distinguishes between truths that hold in all possible worlds and those that do not.

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms

1. **Intensional Fallacy**: While not a neologism, the text's detailed analysis of this fallacy and its application to dualism arguments is a theoretical innovation.
2. **Natural Kinds**: The text's use of the concept of natural kinds to support its arguments about necessity and identity is a theoretical innovation.

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts

1. **Necessity**: The text repurposes the concept of necessity to argue that necessary truths can be non-analytic, using the example of the identity of water and H2O.
2. **Identity**: The text repurposes the concept of identity to argue that if x and y are identical, they must be necessarily so, undermining the concept of contingent identity.

   - **Subtypes**: The text discusses different versions of materialism, including behaviorism, indicating its engagement with various materialist theories.

2. **Dualism**: The text critiques dualism, arguing that it leads to violations of physical law and is therefore untenable.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

1. **Primary Thesis**: The text argues that materialism is the most coherent and defensible position on the mind-body problem, critiquing alternative theories such as dualism, dual aspect theory, eliminativism, and idealism.
   - **Alternative Formulation**: Mental states are physical states, and any theory that posits a non-physical aspect of the mind is untenable.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Argument Against Dualism**: Dualism leads to violations of physical law, which do not occur. Therefore, dualism is false.
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that if dualism were true, mental states would frequently cause violations of physical law, but there is no evidence that such violations occur. This suggests that mental states must be physical states.

2. **Argument for Materialism**: The mental obviously affects the physical, and anything that affects the physical is itself physical.
   - **Explanation**: The text presents Fodor's argument that mental states affect physical states, and therefore must be physical themselves. This supports the materialist view that mental states are physical states.

3. **Critique of Dual Aspect Theory**: Dual aspect theory is incoherent because it cannot specify what the underlying substance is that has both mental and physical aspects.
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that dual aspect theory fails to provide a coherent account of the nature of the substance that supposedly has both mental and physical aspects, leading to the conclusion that it is a misstated form of materialism.

4. **Critique of Eliminativism**: Eliminativism is a fallacious position that denies the existence of mental states, which are clearly experienced and verifiable.
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that eliminativism is based on a misunderstanding of the nature of mental states and their subjective existence, and that it is untenable given the obvious existence of mental phenomena.

5. **Critique of Idealism**: Idealism is not a viable solution to the mind-body problem and is more relevant to epistemological debates about skepticism.
   - **Explanation**: The text dismisses idealism as a view that no one really holds and argues that it does not provide a coherent account of the relationship between mind and body.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Bertrand Russell's View**: Objects are sequences of events governed by physical law, and it makes no sense to speak of a physical object that violates all physical laws.
   - **Explanation**: The text uses Russell's view to argue that when we imagine violations of physical law, we are imagining different physical laws, not non-physical phenomena.

2. **Incoherence of Non-Law-Governed Thoughts**: Thoughts that are not governed by physical laws involve incoherent or impoverished views of physical law.
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that mental states must be governed by physical laws to be coherent, supporting the materialist view that mental states are physical states.

3. **Behaviorism as a Version of Materialism**: According to behaviorism, mental states are dispositions to behave in certain ways, which is a form of materialism.
   - **Explanation**: The text discusses behaviorism as a specific version of materialism, where mental states are equated with behavioral dispositions.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Physicalism**: The text assumes a physicalist worldview, where all phenomena, including mental states, can be explained in terms of physical processes.
   - **Implication**: This assumption underlies the text's arguments against dualism and in favor of materialism.

2. **Empirical Evidence**: The text assumes that empirical evidence is a valid basis for philosophical arguments, particularly in the critique of dualism and the support for materialism.
   - **Implication**: This assumption supports the text's reliance on the lack of evidence for violations of physical law as an argument against dualism.

3. **Coherence as a Criterion**: The text assumes that coherence is a key criterion for evaluating philosophical theories, particularly in the critique of dual aspect theory and eliminativism.
   - **Implication**: This assumption underlies the text's arguments that these theories are incoherent and therefore untenable.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

1. **Premises**: The text begins with premises about the nature of physical law and the relationship between mental and physical states.
   - **Example**: The premise that violations of physical law do not occur is used to argue against dualism.

2. **Inferences**: The text draws inferences from these premises to support its arguments for materialism and against alternative theories.
   - **Example**: The inference that mental states must be physical states because they affect physical states is used to support materialism.

3. **Conclusions**: The text concludes that materialism is the most coherent and defensible position on the mind-body problem.
   - **Example**: The conclusion that dual aspect theory is incoherent leads to the rejection of this theory in favor of materialism.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of Dualism**: The text begins by critiquing dualism, arguing that it leads to violations of physical law.
   - **Development**: The text then presents counter-arguments and counter-responses to further critique dualism.

2. **Argument for Materialism**: The text then presents arguments for materialism, including Fodor's argument that mental states must be physical because they affect physical states.
   - **Development**: The text elaborates on this argument and discusses its implications for the mind-body problem.

3. **Critique of Alternative Theories**: The text then critiques dual aspect theory, eliminativism, and idealism, arguing that they are incoherent or untenable.
   - **Development**: The text provides detailed arguments against each of these theories, showing why they fail to provide a coherent account of the mind-body problem.

4. **Discussion of Materialism Versions**: The text concludes by discussing different versions of materialism, such as behaviorism, to further support its thesis.
   - **Development**: The text explains how these versions of materialism provide a coherent account of mental states as physical states.

## SECTION 4: ANALYTICAL MOVES DEPLOYED

### 4.1 Conceptual Analysis and Distinctions Drawn

   - **Example**: The text distinguishes between dualism and materialism, arguing that dualism leads to violations of physical law while materialism provides a coherent account of mental states.
   - **Function**: These distinctions help to clarify the text's arguments and position within the broader debate.

### 4.2 Argumentation Strategies

1. **Reductio ad Absurdum**: The text uses reductio ad absurdum to argue against dualism, showing that it leads to violations of physical law, which do not occur.
   - **Example**: The text argues that if dualism were true, mental states would frequently cause violations of physical law, but there is no evidence that such violations occur.
   - **Function**: This strategy supports the text's argument against dualism and for materialism.

2. **Modus Tollens**: The text uses modus tollens to argue against dual aspect theory, showing that if the theory were true, it would lead to incoherence, which is not acceptable.
   - **Example**: The text argues that if dual aspect theory were true, it would require a substance that is neither mental nor physical, which is incoherent.
   - **Function**: This strategy supports the text's critique of dual aspect theory and its argument for materialism.

   - **Example**: The text argues that dualism commits a category error by positing mental states that are not governed by physical laws, leading to violations of physical law.
   - **Function**: This detection supports the text's arguments against these theories and for materialism.

## SECTION 1: DOMAIN & DISCIPLINARY CONTEXT

### 1.1 Primary Academic Domain(s) and Subdomain(s)

2. **Cognitive Science**: The discussion of mental states and their causal roles also intersects with cognitive science, particularly in the analysis of how mental states are instantiated in the brain and how they influence behavior.

### 1.2 Interdisciplinary Connections and Influences

1. **Psychology**: The text's focus on behavior and mental states connects to psychology, especially in the critique of behaviorism, which has historical roots in psychological theory.

2. **Neuroscience**: The discussion of brain states and their causal roles in functionalism links to neuroscience, which studies the neural correlates of mental phenomena.

3. **Linguistics**: The example of linguistic knowledge and its role in understanding and responding to questions like "what is 1 + 1?" connects to linguistics, particularly in the study of language acquisition and use.

### 1.3 Historical Positioning Within the Field

1. **Behaviorism**: The text positions itself in the historical context of behaviorism, a theory that was prominent in the early to mid-20th century. It critiques behaviorism's reduction of mental states to behavioral dispositions.

2. **Functionalism**: The text also situates itself within the development of functionalism, which emerged as a response to the shortcomings of behaviorism. Functionalism is presented as a more nuanced approach that considers the causal roles of mental states.

### 1.4 Relationship to Major Schools of Thought

1. **Materialism**: The text engages with materialism, particularly in its discussion of functionalism's attempt to reduce mental states to physical states. The critique of functionalism questions whether it truly achieves this reduction.

2. **Dualism**: While not explicitly mentioned, the text's critique of functionalism implicitly engages with dualism by questioning whether mental states can be fully explained in physical terms.

### 1.5 Target Audience and Scholarly Community

1. **Philosophers of Mind**: The primary audience is philosophers of mind interested in the ongoing debates about the nature of mental states and their relationship to physical states.

2. **Cognitive Scientists**: Cognitive scientists studying the relationship between mental states and behavior would find the text relevant, particularly in its critique of behaviorism and functionalism.

3. **Psychologists**: Psychologists interested in the theoretical foundations of mental states and behavior would also be part of the target audience.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text is that **behaviorism is false and functionalism, while an improvement, also faces significant challenges in explaining the nature of mental states**.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Behaviorism's Failure to Account for Mental States**:
   - Behaviorism reduces mental states to behavioral dispositions, which is inadequate because it fails to consider the role of pre-existing mental states in determining behavior.
   - Example: A person without linguistic knowledge will not respond to "what is 1 + 1?" with "2," demonstrating that behavior is not solely determined by sensory input but also by existing mental states.

2. **Functionalism's Inability to Fully Reduce Mental States to Physical States**:
   - Functionalism identifies mental states with their causal roles, but this approach still relies on other mental states, suggesting that mental states cannot be fully reduced to physical states.
   - Example: The belief that snow is white is defined by its causes (e.g., visual perceptions of white snow) and effects (e.g., forming the belief that snow is not green), which involve other mental states.

3. **The Phenomenal Nature of Mental States**:
   - Mental states, such as pain, have a phenomenal character that cannot be reduced to their causal roles.
   - Example: The feeling of pain is not merely a result of its causes and effects but is an intrinsic experience.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Behaviorism's Oversimplification**:
   - Behaviorism oversimplifies the relationship between sensory input and behavior by ignoring the role of background knowledge and mental states.

2. **Functionalism's Reliance on the Ramsey Sentence**:
   - Functionalists argue that the Ramsey sentence can show that mental states can be reduced to physical states, but this claim is questioned.

3. **The Totality of Mental States**:
   - While individual mental states may be understood in terms of other mental states, the totality of a person's mental states can be understood in terms of physical inputs and behavioral outputs.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Mental States Have Intrinsic Properties**: The text assumes that mental states have intrinsic properties (e.g., the feeling of pain) that cannot be fully captured by their causal roles.

2. **Behavior and Mental States Are Interrelated**: The text assumes that behavior is influenced by both sensory input and pre-existing mental states, challenging the behaviorist view that behavior can be explained solely by sensory input.

3. **Reductionism Is a Desirable Goal**: The text assumes that reducing mental states to physical states is a desirable goal, though it critiques the ability of functionalism to achieve this.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

1. **Critique of Behaviorism**: The text begins by critiquing behaviorism, establishing that mental states cannot be reduced to behavioral dispositions alone.

2. **Introduction of Functionalism**: It then introduces functionalism as a response to behaviorism's shortcomings, explaining how functionalism attempts to account for mental states in terms of their causal roles.

3. **Critique of Functionalism**: The text critiques functionalism, arguing that it fails to fully reduce mental states to physical states and overlooks the phenomenal nature of mental states.

4. **Discussion of the Ramsey Sentence**: Finally, it discusses the functionalist response to these critiques, particularly the use of the Ramsey sentence, and questions its effectiveness.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Initial Critique of Behaviorism**: The text starts by critiquing behaviorism, using examples to illustrate its inadequacies.

2. **Introduction and Explanation of Functionalism**: It then introduces functionalism as a more nuanced approach, explaining how it attempts to address the shortcomings of behaviorism.

3. **Detailed Critique of Functionalism**: The text then delves into a detailed critique of functionalism, focusing on its inability to fully reduce mental states to physical states and its neglect of the phenomenal nature of mental states.

4. **Functionalist Response and Further Critique**: Finally, it discusses the functionalist response to these critiques, particularly the use of the Ramsey sentence, and questions its effectiveness in achieving a reduction of mental states to physical states.

## SECTION 3: REPRESENTATIVE QUOTATIONS (Minimum 10 quotations)

1. **The Author's Main Position**:
   - **Quote**: "Behaviorism is false."
   - **Location/Context**: This statement appears early in the text, setting the stage for the critique of behaviorism.
   - **Significance**: It clearly states the author's rejection of behaviorism, which is a foundational claim for the rest of the argument.

2. **Key Supporting Argument for Behaviorism's Failure**:
   - **Quote**: "How a person reacts to a given sensory input is a function, not only of what that input is, but also of what that person's existing mental states are."
   - **Location/Context**: This appears in the section critiquing behaviorism, explaining why behaviorism fails to account for mental states.
   - **Significance**: It highlights the role of pre-existing mental states in determining behavior, a key point in the critique of behaviorism.

3. **Crucial Definition or Conceptual Distinction**:
   - **Quote**: "If you don't speak English, an utterance of 'what is 1 + 1?' will not cause you to say '2.'"
   - **Location/Context**: This example is used to illustrate the inadequacy of behaviorism in accounting for the role of background knowledge.
   - **Significance**: It provides a concrete example of how behaviorism fails to consider the influence of pre-existing mental states on behavior.

4. **Position Being Criticized or Refuted**:
   - **Quote**: "Behaviorism identifies mental states with dispositions that can be understood in strictly behavioral terms."
   - **Location/Context**: This statement appears in the critique of behaviorism, explaining its reductionist approach.
   - **Significance**: It succinctly captures the behaviorist position that the text aims to refute.

5. **Methodological Statement**:
   - **Quote**: "For any mental category M, there is some causal role C such that x falls into M if and only if x has C."
   - **Location/Context**: This appears in the section explaining functionalism, describing its methodological approach.
   - **Significance**: It outlines the functionalist method of identifying mental states with their causal roles.

6. **Pivotal Turn in the Argument**:
   - **Quote**: "Functionalism says that x is a belief that snow is white iff x has the right causal role."
   - **Location/Context**: This statement marks the transition from the critique of behaviorism to the discussion of functionalism.
   - **Significance**: It introduces the functionalist approach as an alternative to behaviorism, setting the stage for further analysis.

7. **Key Supporting Argument for Functionalism's Failure**:
   - **Quote**: "For something to be a pain is for it to feel a certain way-its causal role is secondary."
   - **Location/Context**: This appears in the critique of functionalism, emphasizing the phenomenal nature of mental states.
   - **Significance**: It highlights a key reason why functionalism fails to fully capture the nature of mental states.

8. **Crucial Definition or Conceptual Distinction**:
   - **Quote**: "The functionalist says that, for any mental type M, mental entities are among the characteristic causes and effects of instances of that type."
   - **Location/Context**: This statement appears in the explanation of functionalism, defining its approach to mental states.
   - **Significance**: It clarifies the functionalist view that mental states are defined by their causal roles, including other mental states.

9. **Position Being Criticized or Refuted**:
   - **Quote**: "Functionalism analyzes the mental into causal role plus the mental: the mental is thus not eliminated-it's still there, making it questionable whether functionalism really is a form of materialism."
   - **Location/Context**: This appears in the critique of functionalism, questioning its ability to reduce mental states to physical states.
   - **Significance**: It challenges the functionalist claim to be a form of materialism by pointing out its reliance on other mental states.

10. **Methodological Statement**:
    - **Quote**: "Functionalists respond by saying that, through judicious use of a technical device known as the 'Ramsey sentence,' it can be shown that functionalism does reduce the mental to the physical, while at the same time doing justice to the fact that, given any arbitrary mental type M, instances of M may have other mental entities for their causes and effects."
    - **Location/Context**: This appears in the section discussing the functionalist response to critiques, explaining their use of the Ramsey sentence.
    - **Significance**: It outlines the functionalist methodological response to the critique that they fail to reduce mental states to physical states.

### 4.2 Argumentation Strategies

   - **What it is**: A form of argument that seeks to disprove a statement by showing that it leads to a contradiction or absurdity.

   - **How it functions in the argument**: This strategy is used to demonstrate the inadequacy of behaviorism by showing that its implications are absurd.

2. **Modus Tollens**:
   - **What it is**: A form of argument that asserts if a certain condition is true, then a particular result follows; if the result is false, then the condition must also be false.
   - **Where it appears**: In the critique of functionalism, where the text argues that if functionalism were true, mental states would be fully reducible to their causal roles; since this is not the case (due to the phenomenal nature of mental states), functionalism must be false.
   - **How it functions in the argument**: This strategy is used to argue against functionalism by showing that its central claim (that mental states are reducible to their causal roles) leads to a false conclusion.

   - **Where it appears**: In the critique of behaviorism, where the text argues that behaviorism commits a category error by reducing mental states (which have intrinsic properties) to behavioral dispositions (which are purely external).

## SECTION 7: CONCEPTUAL APPARATUS

### 7.1 Key Terms and Their Definitions

1. **Behaviorism**: A theory that reduces mental states to behavioral dispositions, where a mental state is defined by the overt behaviors it causes in response to certain sensory inputs.

2. **Functionalism**: A theory that identifies mental states with their causal roles, where a mental state is defined by its characteristic causes and effects, including other mental states.

3. **Mental States**: States of mind that have intrinsic properties and can influence behavior, such as beliefs, desires, and pains.

4. **Causal Role**: The characteristic causes and effects of a mental state, which functionalism uses to define mental states.

5. **Phenomenal Character**: The intrinsic feeling or experience associated with a mental state, such as the feeling of pain.

### 7.2 Technical Vocabulary Introduced or Employed

1. **Disposition**: A tendency to respond with certain overt behaviors to certain sensory inputs, as used in the context of behaviorism.

2. **Ramsey Sentence**: A technical device used by functionalists to show that mental states can be reduced to physical states while still accounting for their causal roles involving other mental states.

### 7.3 Distinctions Drawn Between Related Concepts

1. **Behavioral Dispositions vs. Mental States**: The text distinguishes between behavioral dispositions, which are purely external and based on sensory input and output, and mental states, which have intrinsic properties and pre-existing conditions.

2. **Causal Role vs. Phenomenal Character**: The text also distinguishes between the causal role of mental states, as emphasized by functionalism, and their phenomenal character, which is the intrinsic feeling or experience associated with them.

### 7.4 Theoretical Innovations or Neologisms

1. **No Theoretical Innovations**: The text does not introduce any new theoretical innovations or neologisms but rather critiques existing theories.

### 7.5 Repurposing of Existing Concepts

1. **Causal Role**: The text repurposes the concept of causal role from functionalism to critique its adequacy in explaining mental states, particularly in the context of the phenomenal character of pain.

## SECTION 8: ARGUMENTATIVE STRUCTURE

### 8.1 Overall Organization of the Argument

1. **Introduction and Critique of Behaviorism**: The text begins by introducing and critiquing behaviorism, using examples to illustrate its inadequacies.

2. **Introduction and Explanation of Functionalism**: It then introduces functionalism as a more nuanced approach, explaining how it attempts to address the shortcomings of behaviorism.

3. **Detailed Critique of Functionalism**: The text then delves into a detailed critique of functionalism, focusing on its inability to fully reduce mental states to physical states and its neglect of the phenomenal nature of mental states.

4. **Functionalist Response and Further Critique**: Finally, it discusses the functionalist response to these critiques, particularly the use of the Ramsey sentence, and questions its effectiveness in achieving a reduction of mental states to physical states.

### 8.2 Logical Dependencies Between Claims

1. **Critique of Behaviorism**: The critique of behaviorism depends on the claim that mental states cannot be reduced to behavioral dispositions alone, which is supported by examples like the response to "what is 1 + 1?"

2. **Introduction of Functionalism**: The introduction of functionalism depends on the critique of behaviorism, as it is presented as a response to behaviorism's shortcomings.

3. **Critique of Functionalism**: The critique of functionalism depends on the claim that mental states have a phenomenal character that cannot be reduced to their causal roles, which is supported by the example of pain.

4. **Functionalist Response**: The functionalist response to the critique depends on the use of the Ramsey sentence, which is questioned by the text.

### 8.3 Progression from Premises to Conclusions

1. **Premise**: Behaviorism reduces mental states to behavioral dispositions.
   - **Conclusion**: Behaviorism is false because it fails to account for the role of pre-existing mental states in determining behavior.

2. **Premise**: Functionalism identifies mental states with their causal roles.
   - **Conclusion**: Functionalism is inadequate because it fails to fully reduce mental states to physical states and overlooks their phenomenal nature.

3. **Premise**: Functionalists use the Ramsey sentence to argue that mental states can be reduced to physical states.
   - **Conclusion**: The effectiveness of the Ramsey sentence in achieving this reduction is questionable.

1. **Example of Linguistic Knowledge**: The text uses the example of a person without linguistic knowledge responding to "what is 1 + 1?" to illustrate the inadequacy of behaviorism.

2. **Example of Pain**: The text uses the example of pain to illustrate the phenomenal character of mental states and critique functionalism's focus on causal roles.

2. **Response to Functionalist Objections**: The text responds to potential functionalist objections by arguing that the Ramsey sentence does not fully resolve the issues with functionalism's reduction of mental states to physical states.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The core thesis of the text is that functionalism, as a theory of mind, fails to adequately account for the causal powers of mental states. This thesis can be formulated in multiple ways:

1. **Primary Formulation**: Functionalism cannot explain how mental states cause behavior because it equates mental states with their causal roles, thereby stripping them of independent causal efficacy.
2. **Secondary Formulation**: The mental can be completely described in physical terms without reference to mental kinds, but functionalism's approach to mental content is too brittle and fails to capture the causal interactions between mental states.

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Argument 1: The Mental Can Be Described Physically Without Mental Kinds**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that for any mental kind, one can describe its causes and effects in purely physical terms by replacing mental kind expressions with variables and binding them with existential quantifiers. This process results in a sentence that describes the mental in physical terms without using mental expressions, suggesting that the mental can be fully accounted for physically.

2. **Argument 2: Functionalism Makes Content Too Brittle**
   - **Explanation**: The text critiques functionalism for making mental content too dependent on exact causal roles. It argues that if content is determined by causal role, no two mental states can have the same content because no two states have exactly the same causal role. This leads to the absurd conclusion that no two people can share the same belief, which the text deems false.

3. **Argument 3: Functionalism Strips Mental States of Causal Powers**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that functionalism equates a mental state with its causal role, which means the state cannot cause anything because cause and effect must be distinct. This leads to the conclusion that, according to functionalism, mental states cannot cause behavior, which contradicts the evident causal powers of mental states.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Subsidiary Claim 1: Causal Role Does Not Fix Content**
   - **Explanation**: The text uses the example of Smith and Jones, who both believe that snow is white but have different functional roles for this belief due to their different backgrounds. This supports the claim that causal role alone cannot determine content.

2. **Subsidiary Claim 2: States of Affairs, Not Objects, Have Causal Properties**
   - **Explanation**: The text argues that it is not objects but states of affairs that have causal properties. This is used to critique functionalism's focus on brain-states as the bearers of causal roles.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Assumption 1: Mental States Have Causal Powers**
   - **Explanation**: The text assumes that mental states do have causal powers, which is a foundational assumption for its critique of functionalism.

2. **Assumption 2: Cause and Effect Must Be Distinct**
   - **Explanation**: The argument that functionalism strips mental states of causal powers relies on the assumption that cause and effect must be distinct entities.

3. **Assumption 3: Mental States Can Be Described Physically**
   - **Explanation**: The text's method of describing mental states in physical terms without using mental expressions assumes that such a description is possible and meaningful.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The text's overall argument follows a deductive structure, moving from general principles about the nature of mental states and their causal roles to specific critiques of functionalism:

1. **Premise 1**: Mental states can be described in physical terms without reference to mental kinds.
2. **Premise 2**: Functionalism equates mental states with their causal roles.
3. **Premise 3**: Cause and effect must be distinct.
4. **Conclusion**: Functionalism fails to account for the causal powers of mental states because it equates them with their causal roles, thereby stripping them of independent causal efficacy.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Introduction of the Method**: The text begins by introducing a method for describing mental states in physical terms, setting the stage for its critique of functionalism.
2. **Critique of Functionalism's Brittleness**: It then critiques functionalism for making content too dependent on exact causal roles, using the example of Smith and Jones to illustrate this point.
3. **Causal Powers Argument**: The text then develops its main argument that functionalism strips mental states of causal powers by equating them with their causal roles.
4. **Conclusion**: The text concludes by reaffirming its critique of functionalism and suggesting that materialism, while promising, faces challenges from the apparent differences between mental and physical phenomena.

## SECTION 3: REPRESENTATIVE QUOTATIONS

1. **Quote 1**: "single, very large sentence that says, for any given mental kind (e.g., belief that snow is white, dread of visiting one's mother in law, etc.), what the causes and effects of instances of that kind are."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears at the beginning of the text, setting up the method for describing mental states in physical terms.
   - **Significance**: It introduces the text's approach to describing mental states and foreshadows its critique of functionalism by suggesting that mental states can be fully accounted for in physical terms.

2. **Quote 2**: "MS is going to contain 'constants': it will contain expressions, such as 'dread of going jogging in the morning,' that refer to kinds of mental entities."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote follows the initial method description, explaining how mental states are represented in the text's approach.
   - **Significance**: It clarifies the text's method by specifying that mental states are treated as constants that can be replaced with variables for physical description.

3. **Quote 3**: "The result will be a sentence that completely describes the causes and effects of instances of each mental kind, but that does so without using any expressions that refer to anything mental."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears after the method is fully described, emphasizing the outcome of the process.
   - **Significance**: It underscores the text's claim that mental states can be fully described in physical terms, setting the stage for its critique of functionalism.

4. **Quote 4**: "By saying that causal role fixes content, RS makes content too brittle: any deviations of any kind between any two mental states ipso facto amount to differences in content."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the critique of functionalism, specifically addressing the theory's approach to mental content.
   - **Significance**: It highlights the text's argument that functionalism's reliance on causal roles to determine content leads to an overly rigid and unrealistic view of mental states.

5. **Quote 5**: "No two mental entities have exactly the same causal roles. (There isn't anything in your mind that has exactly the same causal role as my belief that 2 is an even prime. There isn't anything in any person's mind that has exactly the same causal role as anything in anyone else's mind.)"
   - **Location/Context**: This quote follows the critique of functionalism's brittleness, providing evidence for the claim.
   - **Significance**: It supports the text's argument that functionalism's approach to content leads to the absurd conclusion that no two people can share the same belief.

6. **Quote 6**: "It follows that, if RS is right, no two people can believe that 1 + 1 = 2 or that Mongolia is in Asia, etc. Since that is obviously false, so is RS."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote concludes the critique of functionalism's brittleness, drawing a logical consequence from the argument.
   - **Significance**: It emphasizes the text's rejection of functionalism by showing that its implications are clearly false.

7. **Quote 7**: "Both Smith and Jones believe that snow is white. Smith is a physicist who focuses on the reflective properties of white surfaces. Jones doesn't know anything about physics. Obviously the functional role of Smith's belief that snow is white will be very different from that of Jones' corresponding belief."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in a story used to illustrate the critique of functionalism's brittleness.
   - **Significance**: It provides a concrete example to support the text's argument that functionalism's reliance on causal roles leads to an overly narrow view of mental content.

8. **Quote 8**: "It is not objects, but states of affairs, that have causal properties. What shatters the window is not the rock-it is rather the rock's moving with a certain velocity, in a certain direction, etc."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote introduces the argument that states of affairs, not objects, have causal properties.
   - **Significance**: It sets up the text's critique of functionalism's focus on brain-states as the bearers of causal roles, emphasizing the importance of states of affairs in causation.

9. **Quote 9**: "According to functionalism, B's being identical with a belief that water quenches thirst is identical with B's having certain causes and effects-it is identical with (e.g.) its causing you to drink water when thirsty."
   - **Location/Context**: This quote appears in the argument that functionalism strips mental states of causal powers.
   - **Significance**: It articulates the text's critique of functionalism by showing how the theory equates mental states with their causal roles, thereby undermining their causal efficacy.

10. **Quote 10**: "So if functionalism is correct, then the fact that you believe that water quenches thirst cannot cause you to do things like drink water when thirsty. Thus, if functionalism is right, then your belief that water quenches thirst does nothing."
    - **Location/Context**: This quote concludes the argument that functionalism strips mental states of causal powers.
    - **Significance**: It emphasizes the text's main thesis that functionalism fails to account for the causal powers of mental states, leading to the conclusion that the theory is incorrect.

## SECTION 8: ARGUMENTATIVE STRUCTURE

### 8.1 Overall Organization of the Argument

1. **Introduction of the Method**: The text begins by introducing a method for describing mental states in physical terms, setting the stage for its critique of functionalism.
2. **Critique of Functionalism's Brittleness**: It then critiques functionalism for making content too dependent on exact causal roles, using the example of Smith and Jones to illustrate this point.
3. **Causal Powers Argument**: The text then develops its main argument that functionalism strips mental states of causal powers by equating them with their causal roles.
4. **Conclusion**: The text concludes by reaffirming its critique of functionalism and suggesting that materialism, while promising, faces challenges from the apparent differences between mental and physical phenomena.

### 8.2 Logical Dependencies Between Claims

1. **Mental States Can Be Described Physically**: The text's method of describing mental states in physical terms is a foundational claim that supports its critique of functionalism.

3. **Functionalism Strips Mental States of Causal Powers**: The argument that functionalism strips mental states of causal powers depends on the critique of functionalism's brittleness and the claim that cause and effect must be distinct.

### 8.3 Progression from Premises to Conclusions

1. **Premise 1**: Mental states can be described in physical terms without reference to mental kinds.
2. **Premise 2**: Functionalism equates mental states with their causal roles.
3. **Premise 3**: Cause and effect must be distinct.
4. **Conclusion**: Functionalism fails to account for the causal powers of mental states because it equates them with their causal roles, thereby stripping them of independent causal efficacy.

### 8.4 Use of Examples, Illustrations, or Case Studies

2. **Rock and Window Analogy**: The text uses the analogy of a rock shattering a window to illustrate the point that it is states of affairs, not objects, that have causal properties.

1. **Objection to Functionalism's Brittleness**: The text anticipates the objection that functionalism's approach to mental content is too rigid and responds by showing that it leads to the absurd conclusion that no two people can share the same belief.
2. **Objection to Functionalism's Causal Powers**: The text anticipates the objection that functionalism strips mental states of causal powers and responds by arguing that this is incompatible with our experience of mental causation.

## SECTION 2: MAIN THESIS & CENTRAL ARGUMENTS

### 2.1 Core Thesis Statement

The text does not present a single overarching thesis but rather engages with multiple philosophical questions and arguments. However, if we were to synthesize a central theme, it could be formulated as:

- **The nature of knowledge, particularly self-knowledge and knowledge of the external world, is complex and involves significant inferential and theoretical structure, while the relationship between mind and body remains a contentious issue in philosophy.**

### 2.2 Primary Arguments Supporting the Thesis

1. **Descartes' Wax Argument**
   - **Argument**: Descartes' wax argument demonstrates that our knowledge of the external world, even of seemingly simple objects like wax, involves a highly theoretical and inferential structure.
   - **Explanation**: Descartes argues that we recognize the same piece of wax despite its changing physical properties (e.g., from solid to liquid) because we understand it through its essential properties, not its sensory qualities. This suggests that our knowledge of the external world is not merely empirical but involves rational understanding.

2. **Complexity of Self-Knowledge**
   - **Argument**: Self-knowledge, like knowledge of the external world, has a highly theoretical character and involves inferential structure.
   - **Explanation**: The text suggests that understanding oneself involves more than direct introspection; it requires theoretical and inferential processes, similar to how we understand the external world.

3. **Mind-Body Dualism vs. Materialism**
   - **Argument**: The debate between mind-body dualism and materialism highlights the complexity of understanding the relationship between mental and physical states.
   - **Explanation**: Descartes' argument for dualism posits that the mind and body are distinct substances, while materialism argues that mental states are reducible to physical states. This debate underscores the difficulty in reconciling our subjective experiences with the physical world.

### 2.3 Secondary Arguments and Subsidiary Claims

1. **Foundationalism and Coherentism**
   - **Argument**: The text discusses foundationalism and coherentism as competing theories of knowledge, with implications for understanding the nature of self-evident truths.
   - **Explanation**: Foundationalism posits that knowledge is built on a foundation of self-evident truths, while coherentism argues that knowledge is a coherent system of beliefs. The text suggests that a philosopher like Smith, who believes in a small number of self-evident truths, aligns with foundationalism.

2. **Belief and Identity**
   - **Argument**: The text argues that belief statements about identity (e.g., water and H2O) involve complex semantic and logical considerations.
   - **Explanation**: The example of Smith believing that water is identical with water but not necessarily with H2O highlights the distinction between belief and factual identity, suggesting that belief statements do not always align with objective truths.

### 2.4 Implicit Assumptions Underlying the Arguments

1. **Rationalist Epistemology**
   - The text assumes that reason plays a crucial role in understanding both the self and the external world, as evidenced by the discussion of Descartes' wax argument.

2. **Semantic Realism**
   - The text assumes that there is a distinction between belief and reality, as seen in the discussion of Smith's beliefs about water and H2O.

3. **Substance Dualism**
   - The text assumes the possibility of substance dualism, as it presents Descartes' argument for the distinction between mind and body without challenging its coherence.

### 2.5 Logical Structure of the Overall Argument

The text does not present a single, unified argument but rather a series of interconnected philosophical discussions. The logical structure can be broken down as follows:

1. **Introduction of Key Concepts**
   - The text begins by introducing key concepts such as self-knowledge, knowledge of the external world, mind-body dualism, and materialism.

2. **Presentation of Arguments**
   - Each section presents a specific argument or set of arguments related to the introduced concepts, such as Descartes' wax argument, the complexity of self-knowledge, and the debate between dualism and materialism.

3. **Analysis and Implications**
   - The text then analyzes these arguments, discussing their implications for understanding knowledge and the mind-body relationship.

4. **Counterarguments and Objections**
   - The text anticipates and responds to potential objections, such as the logical principle invoked in the discussion of belief and identity.

### 2.6 Argument Progression and Development Throughout the Text

1. **Initial Focus on Knowledge**
   - The text begins by discussing the nature of knowledge, particularly self-knowledge and knowledge of the external world, using Descartes' wax argument as a key example.

2. **Expansion to Epistemological Theories**
   - The discussion then expands to broader epistemological theories, such as foundationalism and coherentism, to contextualize the nature of self-evident truths.

3. **Shift to Metaphysical Concerns**
   - The text shifts focus to metaphysical questions, presenting the debate between mind-body dualism and materialism, and providing arguments for each position.

4. **Integration of Semantic and Logical Analysis**
